Course overview

Data science life cycle

Yu and Kumbier, PNAS’19

Course content

  1. Advanced R
  • dplyr, transforming vectors, numbers, strings, factors, dates, times
  • ggplot visualizations
  • Functions
  1. Correlation, regression, and inference
  • Linear regression
  • Analysis of variance
  • Permutation tests

Prerequisite(s): one of the following two options:

  • STA 013 (C- or better) or STA 013Y (C- or better) or ECS 032A (C- or better)
  • STA 035A (C- or better) or STA 032 (C- or better) or STA 100 (C- or better)

NOTE: If you only took STA 13, this will be a difficult course!

Corequisite (i.e., can be concurrent):

  • MAT 016B or MAT 017B or MAT 021B.

These requirements are strict.

R programming prerequisite(s)

R-related topics covered in prerequisites:

  • STA 35A: Overview of data types and data structures, vectors, arrays, matrices, lists, data frames, exploratory data analysis, data manipulation tools, data visualization tools
  • STA 32: Vectors, matrices, data frames, functions, plotting and printing functions, data input and output, logical control statements, loops
  • STA 100: Implementations in the R environment

Roughly equivalent to

  • Chapters 1-5, 11 of A Sufficient Introduction to R, or
  • R Basics from UC Davis DataLab

We will review some but not all of these prerequisites

Tips on succeeding in this course

Example default weekly schedule (6 hours/week outside of class)

  • Thursday: finish lab during lab section
  • Friday-Monday: do homework (3 hour)
  • Tuesday: study homework solutions (1 hour)

This leaves 1 hour for office hours, extra studying, etc.

  • Could even schedule specific time blocks as recurring events in calendar

Rule of thumb when studying for exams: can you do problems without looking at notes?

Software

Software

  • R is a free, open-source statistical programming language for statistical computing
  • It is also an interactive environment for doing data science
  • Data science teams often use a mix of languages, including R, Python, Julia, …
  • Typically better to “master” one language before moving on to learning another
  • R Console: Basic interaction with R is by typing in the console, a.k.a. terminal or command-line
  • You type in commands, R gives back answers (or errors)
  • It is easily extensible with packages
  • Menus and other graphical interfaces are extras built on top of the console

Quick R demonstration

  • Arithmetic
1 + 2
[1] 3
  • Comparisons
1 == 2
[1] FALSE

Computing set up

  • UC Davis JupyterHub has RStudio set up

  • Alternatively, you can install R and RStudio on your own computers

  • You will need regular, reliable access to a computer either with a working browser, or running an up-to-date version of R and RStudio

  • If this is a problem, please let us know right away. There are resources available to support you.

  • Labs will be at TLC 2212; either use computers available in the lab, or your own laptops (make sure your laptop is charged before class)

JupyterHub

After going to Jupyter hub and clicking on RStudio, it will bring you to this page:

To upload qmd files or data files, download them to your computer (from the course webpage or from Canvas), and then upload them to the server using the Upload button in the Files pane.

A short list of R essentials

  • Functions are (most often) verbs, followed by what they will be applied to in parentheses:
do_this(to_this)
do_that(to_this, to_that, with_those)

  • Packages are extensions to R

    • They include functions, documentation, and sample data

    • They are hosted on CRAN (the Comprehensive R Archive Network)

    • They are installed using install.packages() and loaded using library(), once per session:

install.packages("package_name")
library(package_name)

Alternatively, you can call a function from a package without loading it, using package_name::do_this().

A short list of R essentials

  • Object documentation can be accessed with ? or help()
?mean

  • The environment is where R finds the value associated with a name

  • Comments are lines or text that start with the hash #

    • These will not be executed

Homework 0 (to be assigned this week)

Pick either R markdown or Quarto. See course website for homework file.

  • R markdown tutorial

  • Quarto tutorial

(Hint: start this before your lab section this week so that you can more easily get in-person help from your TA.)

R Markdown

  • R Markdown is a tool to integrate code and written prose in reproducible computational documents

  • R Markdown files have the rmd extension. Each time you “Knit,” the analysis is run from the beginning.

  • To learn more, go to rmarkdown.rstudio.com

  • Labs will be completed in R Markdown (or Quarto)

  • Code goes in chunks, defined by three backticks, narrative goes outside of chunks

Quarto

  • Quarto is a tool to integrate code and written prose in reproducible computational documents

  • Quarto files have the qmd extension. Each time you “Render,” the analysis is run from the beginning.

  • To learn more, go to quarto.org

  • Labs will be completed in Quarto (or R Markdown)

  • Code goes in chunks, defined by three backticks, narrative goes outside of chunks

Keyboard shortcuts

Windows (Mac: same but replace Ctrl with command)

  • Ctrl+z : Undo
  • Ctrl+x : Cut
  • Ctrl+c : Copy
  • Ctrl+v : Paste
  • Ctrl+f : Find (sometimes also Find + Replace)
  • Ctrl+a : Select all text in the file
  • Ctrl+s : Save file
  • Ctrl+return or Ctrl+Enter : Execute line of code that cursor is on
  • Ctrl+Left arrow : Move cursor to the beginning of the line
  • Ctrl+Right arrow : Move cursor to the end of the line

Fundamentals of R

Assign values to variables

You assign values to variables using the <- operator.

x <- 10.5
x
[1] 10.5

Parentheses will print value out after assignment.

(x <- 11.5)  
[1] 11.5

The = operator is fine too.

y = 10.5
y
[1] 10.5

The <- operator is preferred by the R style guides from Tidyverse and Google.

Basic data types in R

Basic data types:

  • Numeric (235.22)
  • Integer (1)
  • Character / string ("You are here")
  • Logical / boolean (TRUE, FALSE)
  • Missing (NA)
x <- 10.5
class(x)
[1] "numeric"
x <- 1000L # R declares integers by putting L after numbers
class(x)
[1] "integer"
x <- "I love programming"
class(x)
[1] "character"
x <- TRUE
class(x)
[1] "logical"

Inspect data

You can inspect data by entering the variable name into the console, or by using str()

x <- 7
x
[1] 7
str(x)
 num 7
y <- 7L
y
[1] 7
str(y)
 int 7

What is the difference between

  • the output of x and y?
  • the output of str(x) and str(y)?

Combining values into vectors

You can combine values into a vector using c().

x <- c(3, 2)
str(x)
 num [1:2] 3 2

c() will sometimes coerce different data types into the same type.

x <- c(3, "haha")
str(x)
 chr [1:2] "3" "haha"

You can combine c() with rep(thing_to_repeat, num_repetitions):

rep(x, 3)
[1] "3"    "haha" "3"    "haha" "3"    "haha"
rep(c(6, 2), 4)
[1] 6 2 6 2 6 2 6 2

Combining values into list

(What is the difference between a list and a vector?)

Combine values into a list using list().

x <- list(3, "haha")
str(x)
List of 2
 $ : num 3
 $ : chr "haha"
y <- c(x, 5.4)
str(y)
List of 3
 $ : num 3
 $ : chr "haha"
 $ : num 5.4

Can also use rep(thing_to_repeat, num_repetitions):

z <- rep(y, 2)
str(z)
List of 6
 $ : num 3
 $ : chr "haha"
 $ : num 5.4
 $ : num 3
 $ : chr "haha"
 $ : num 5.4

Data frames and tibbles

Most data sets come in the form of dataframes and tibbles - you should be familiar with these.

  • Many datasets are built into base R
  • Commonly used examples: USArrests, PlantGrowth, ToothGrowth, mtcars
  • Useful for trying out functions on real (and clean) datasets
  • Saves you the trouble of collecting data yourself
  • Packages have additional datasets (typically larger)

To return data from a column in a data frame

mtcars is a data frame.

df$COLUMN

mtcars$mpg  # returns vector
 [1] 21.0 21.0 22.8 21.4 18.7 18.1 14.3 24.4 22.8 19.2 17.8 16.4 17.3 15.2 10.4
[16] 10.4 14.7 32.4 30.4 33.9 21.5 15.5 15.2 13.3 19.2 27.3 26.0 30.4 15.8 19.7
[31] 15.0 21.4

df[, "COLUMN"]

mtcars[, "mpg"]  # returns vector
 [1] 21.0 21.0 22.8 21.4 18.7 18.1 14.3 24.4 22.8 19.2 17.8 16.4 17.3 15.2 10.4
[16] 10.4 14.7 32.4 30.4 33.9 21.5 15.5 15.2 13.3 19.2 27.3 26.0 30.4 15.8 19.7
[31] 15.0 21.4

To return data from a column in a dataframe/tibble

Can also access columns using integer indices using df[, column_number]

# mtcars is a data frame
mtcars[, 2]  # returns 2nd column of mtcars as a vector
 [1] 6 6 4 6 8 6 8 4 4 6 6 8 8 8 8 8 8 4 4 4 4 8 8 8 8 4 4 4 8 6 8 4
# flights is a tibble
flights[, 2]  # returns 2nd column of flights as a tibble
# A tibble: 336,776 × 1
   month
   <int>
 1     1
 2     1
 3     1

 5     1

flights[, c(4,5)] would also work

To return data from a row in a dataframe

df[row_number, ]

mtcars[1, ]  # returns 1st row of mtcars as a data.frame
          mpg cyl disp  hp drat   wt  qsec vs am gear carb
Mazda RX4  21   6  160 110  3.9 2.62 16.46  0  1    4    4
str(mtcars[1, ])
'data.frame':   1 obs. of  11 variables:
 $ mpg : num 21
 $ cyl : num 6
 $ disp: num 160
 $ hp  : num 110
 $ drat: num 3.9
 $ wt  : num 2.62
 $ qsec: num 16.5
 $ vs  : num 0
 $ am  : num 1
 $ gear: num 4
 $ carb: num 4

To return data from a row in a tibble

df[row_number, ]
